           <h1 name="a3b3" id="a3b3" class="graf graf--h3 graf--leading graf--title">Your Node.js authentication tutorial is (probably) wrong</h1>
           <p name="e487" id="e487" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">tl;dr: </strong>I went on a search of Node.js/Express.js authentication tutorials. All of them were incomplete or made a security mistake in some way that can potentially hurt new users. This post explores some common authentication pitfalls, how to avoid them, and what to do to help yourself when your tutorials don’t help you anymore. I am still searching for a robust, all-in-one solution for authentication in Node/Express that rivals Rails’s <a href="https://github.com/plataformatec/devise" data-href="https://github.com/plataformatec/devise" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Devise</a>.</p>
           <blockquote name="4af9" id="4af9" class="graf graf--blockquote graf-after--p">
            <strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Update (Aug 7)</strong>: RisingStack has reached out and 
            <a href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/commit/9d69ea70b68c4971466c64382e5f038e3eda8d8a" data-href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/commit/9d69ea70b68c4971466c64382e5f038e3eda8d8a" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">no longer stores passwords in plaintext</a> in their tutorial, opting to move to bcrypt in their example codes and tutorials.
            <br>
            <strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Update (Aug 8):</strong> Editing title to 
            <em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em">Your Node.js authentication tutorial is (probably) wrong</em>, as this post has improved some of these tutorials.
            <br>
            <strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Update (Aug 10): </strong>
            <a href="https://twitter.com/dmcghan" data-href="https://twitter.com/dmcghan" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em">Dan McGhan</em></a>
            <em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em"> found that one of the tutorials has addressed an issue that I had somehow missed in this documentation. I’ve omitted the graf for now, as Medium doesn’t allow for strikethrough. After all, I make mistakes, too. ? An addendum is placed at the end of this article.</em>
           </blockquote>
           <p name="7345" id="7345" class="graf graf--p graf--hasDropCapModel graf--hasDropCap graf-after--blockquote"><span class="graf-dropCap">In</span> my spare time, I’ve been digging through various Node.js tutorials, as it seems that every Node.js developer with a blog has released their own tutorial on how to do things <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">the right way</em>, or, more accurately, <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">the way they do them</em>. Thousands of front-end developers being thrown into the server-side JS maelstrom are trying to piece together actionable knowledge from these tutorials, either by cargo-cult-copypasta or gratuitous use of <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">npm install </em>as they scramble frantically to meet the deadlines set for them by outsourcing managers or ad agency creative directors.</p>
           <p name="2b07" id="2b07" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">One of the more questionable things in Node.js development is that authentication is largely left as an exercise to the individual developer<em class="markup--em markup--p-em">. </em>The <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">de facto </em>authentication solution in the Express.js world is <a href="http://passportjs.org/" data-href="http://passportjs.org/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Passport</a>, which offers a host of <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">strategies</em> for authentication. If you want a robust solution similar to <a href="https://github.com/plataformatec/devise" data-href="https://github.com/plataformatec/devise" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Plataformatec’s Devise</a> for Ruby on Rails, you’ll likely be pointed to <a href="https://auth0.com/" data-href="https://auth0.com/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Auth0</a>, a startup who has made authentication as a service.</p>
           <p name="2674" id="2674" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Compared to Devise, Passport is simply authentication middleware, and does not handle any of the other parts of authentication for you: that means the Node.js developer is likely to roll their own API token mechanisms, password reset token mechanisms, user authentication routes and endpoints, and views in whatever templating language is the rage today. Because of this, there are a lot of tutorials that specialize in setting up Passport for your Express.js application, and nearly all of them are wrong in some way or another, and none properly implement the full stack necessary for a working web application.</p>
           <blockquote name="b723" id="b723" class="graf graf--blockquote graf-after--p">
            <strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Note</strong>: I’m not attempting to harass the developers of these tutorials specifically, but rather I am using their authentication mistakes to show security issues inherent in rolling your own authentication systems. If you are a tutorial writer, feel free to reach out to me once you’ve updated your tutorial. Let’s make Node/Express a safer ecosystem for new developers.
           </blockquote>
           <h3 name="2afb" id="2afb" class="graf graf--h3 graf-after--blockquote">Mistake one: credential storage</h3>
           <p name="1cb4" id="1cb4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">Let’s start with credential storage. Storing and recalling credentials is pretty standard fare for identity management, and the traditional way to do this is in your own database or application. Passport, being middleware that simply says “this user is cool” or “this user is not cool”, requires the <a href="https://github.com/jaredhanson/passport-local" data-href="https://github.com/jaredhanson/passport-local" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">passport-local</a> module for handling password storage in your own database, written by the same developer as Passport.js itself.</p>
           <p name="0582" id="0582" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Before we go down this tutorial rabbit hole, let’s remind ourselves of a <a href="https://www.owasp.org/index.php/Password_Storage_Cheat_Sheet" data-href="https://www.owasp.org/index.php/Password_Storage_Cheat_Sheet" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">great cheat sheet for password storage</a> by OWASP, which boils down to “store high-entropy passwords with unique salts and one-way adaptive cost functions”. Or, really, Coda Hale’s <a href="https://codahale.com/how-to-safely-store-a-password/" data-href="https://codahale.com/how-to-safely-store-a-password/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">bcrypt meme</a>, even though <a href="https://security.stackexchange.com/a/6415" data-href="https://security.stackexchange.com/a/6415" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">there’s some contention</a>.</p>
           <blockquote name="90d1" id="90d1" class="graf graf--blockquote graf-after--p">
            <strong class="markup--strong markup--blockquote-strong">Note for experienced developers:</strong> If you 
            <em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em">really</em> want to work with state of the art credential storage, 
            <a href="https://github.com/P-H-C/phc-winner-argon2" data-href="https://github.com/P-H-C/phc-winner-argon2" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em">Argon2</em></a>
            <em class="markup--em markup--blockquote-em"> </em>is the winner of the Password Hashing Competition and now has 
            <a href="https://github.com/emilbayes/secure-password" data-href="https://github.com/emilbayes/secure-password" class="markup--anchor markup--blockquote-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">some easy support in Node.js</a>. Unfortunately, documentation for the implementation of Argon2 for novice users in the Node.js ecosystem is still lacking, and given that this is targeted toward people reading tutorials, sticking with bcrypt for the next few months seemed an OK tradeoff.
           </blockquote>
           <p name="41a4" id="41a4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--blockquote">As a new Express.js and Passport user, my first place to look will be the example code for <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">passport-local</em> itself, which <a href="https://github.com/passport/express-4.x-local-example" data-href="https://github.com/passport/express-4.x-local-example" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">thankfully gives me a sample Express.js 4.0 application</a> I can clone and extend. However, if I just copypasta this, I’m not left with too much, as there’s no database support in the example and it assumes I’m just using some set accounts.</p>
           <p name="a4a8" id="a4a8" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">That’s OK, though, right? <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">It’s just an Intranet application</em>, the dev says, <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">and I have four other projects assigned to me due next week</em>. Of course, the passwords for the example aren’t hashed in any way, <a href="https://github.com/passport/express-4.x-local-example/blob/master/db/users.js" data-href="https://github.com/passport/express-4.x-local-example/blob/master/db/users.js" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">and stored in plaintext right alongside the validation logic in this example</a>. Credential storage isn’t even considered in this one.</p>
           <p name="7a86" id="7a86" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Let’s google for another tutorial using <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">passport-local</em>. I find<a href="https://blog.risingstack.com/node-hero-node-js-authentication-passport-js/" data-href="https://blog.risingstack.com/node-hero-node-js-authentication-passport-js/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"> this quick tutorial from RisingStack in a series</a> called “Node Hero”, but that doesn’t help me, either. They, too, <a href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication" data-href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">gave me a sample application on GitHub</a>, but it had <a href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/blob/7f808f5c8ea756155099b7b4a88390c356cf31be/app/authentication/init.js#L8" data-href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/blob/7f808f5c8ea756155099b7b4a88390c356cf31be/app/authentication/init.js#L8" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">the same problems as the official one</a>. <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">(<em class="markup--em markup--p-em">Ed. 8/7/17: </em>RisingStack is<em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> </em></strong><a href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/commit/9d69ea70b68c4971466c64382e5f038e3eda8d8a" data-href="https://github.com/RisingStack/nodehero-authentication/commit/9d69ea70b68c4971466c64382e5f038e3eda8d8a" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">now using bcrypt</strong></a><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong"> in their tutorial application.)</strong></p>
           <p name="87c8" id="87c8" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">You could accuse me of cherry-picking tutorials, and you’d be right, if cherry picking means selecting from the first page of Google results. Let’s choose the <a href="https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/authenticating-nodejs-applications-with-passport--cms-21619" data-href="https://code.tutsplus.com/tutorials/authenticating-nodejs-applications-with-passport--cms-21619" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">higher-ranked-in-results <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">passport-local</em> tutorial from TutsPlus</a>. This one is better, in that <a href="https://github.com/tutsplus/passport-mongo/blob/master/passport/login.js" data-href="https://github.com/tutsplus/passport-mongo/blob/master/passport/login.js" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">it uses brypt with a cost factor of 10 for password hashing</a>. The top result on Google, <a href="https://scotch.io/tutorials/easy-node-authentication-setup-and-local" data-href="https://scotch.io/tutorials/easy-node-authentication-setup-and-local" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">the tutorial from scotch.io</a>, also uses <a href="https://github.com/scotch-io/easy-node-authentication/blob/local/app/models/user.js#L37" data-href="https://github.com/scotch-io/easy-node-authentication/blob/local/app/models/user.js#L37" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">bcrypt with a lesser cost factor of 8</a>. Both of these are small, but 8 is really small. Most <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">bcrypt</em> libraries these days use 12. <a href="https://www.usenix.org/legacy/publications/library/proceedings/usenix99/provos/provos_html/node6.html" data-href="https://www.usenix.org/legacy/publications/library/proceedings/usenix99/provos/provos_html/node6.html" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">The cost factor of 8 was for administrator accounts <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">eighteen years ago</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> </em>when the original bcrypt paper was released.</p>
           <p name="df3b" id="df3b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Password storage aside, neither of these tutorials implement password reset functionality, which is left as an exercise to the developer and comes with its own pitfalls.</p>
           <h3 name="17ff" id="17ff" class="graf graf--h3 graf-after--p">Mistake two: password&nbsp;reset</h3>
           <p name="88ac" id="88ac" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">A sister security issue to password storage is that of password reset, and none of the top basic tutorials explain how to do this at all with Passport. You’ll have to follow another.</p>
           <p name="b01d" id="b01d" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">There are a thousand ways to fuck this up. The most common ways I have witnessed that people get password reset wrong are:</p>
           <ol class="postList">
            <li name="d12b" id="d12b" class="graf graf--li graf-after--p"><strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">Predictable tokens. </strong>Tokens that are based upon the current time are a good example. Tokens made by bad pseudorandom number generators are less obvious.</li>
            <li name="2f40" id="2f40" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li"><strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">Bad storage. </strong>Storing unencrypted password reset tokens in your DB means that if the DB is compromised, those tokens are effectively plaintext passwords. Generating a long token with a cryptographically secure random number generator stops remote brute force attacks on reset tokens, but it doesn’t stop local attacks. Reset tokens are credentials and should be treated as such.</li>
            <li name="f6a1" id="f6a1" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li"><strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">No token expiry. </strong>Not expiring your tokens gives attackers more time to exploit the reset window.</li>
            <li name="655b" id="655b" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li"><strong class="markup--strong markup--li-strong">No secondary data verification.</strong> Security questions are the <em class="markup--em markup--li-em">de facto</em> data verification for a reset. Of course, then the developer has to choose <em class="markup--em markup--li-em">good security questions</em>. <a href="https://www.kaspersky.com/blog/security-questions-are-insecure/13004/" data-href="https://www.kaspersky.com/blog/security-questions-are-insecure/13004/" class="markup--anchor markup--li-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Security questions have their own problems</a>. While this may seem like security overkill, the email address is something you have, not something you know, and conflates the authentication factors. Your email address becomes the key to every account that just sends a reset token to email.</li>
           </ol>
           <p name="fa78" id="fa78" class="graf graf--p graf-after--li">If you’re new all of this, try OWASP’s <a href="https://www.owasp.org/index.php/Forgot_Password_Cheat_Sheet" data-href="https://www.owasp.org/index.php/Forgot_Password_Cheat_Sheet" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Password Reset Cheat Sheet</a>. Let’s get back to what the Node world has to offer for us on this.</p>
           <p name="338c" id="338c" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">We’ll divert to <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">npm </em>for a second and <a href="https://www.npmjs.com/search?q=password%20reset&amp;page=1&amp;ranking=popularity" data-href="https://www.npmjs.com/search?q=password%20reset&amp;page=1&amp;ranking=popularity" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">look for password reset</a>, to see if anyone’s made this. There’s a five-year-old package from the (generally awesome) substack. On the Node.js timeline this module is jurassic, and if I wanted to nitpick, <a href="https://security.stackexchange.com/questions/84906/predicting-math-random-numbers" data-href="https://security.stackexchange.com/questions/84906/predicting-math-random-numbers" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Math.random() is predictable in V8</a>, so <a href="https://github.com/substack/node-password-reset/blob/master/index.js#L73" data-href="https://github.com/substack/node-password-reset/blob/master/index.js#L73" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">it shouldn’t be used for token generation</a>. Also, it doesn’t use Passport, so we’ll move on.</p>
           <p name="c14d" id="c14d" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Stack Overflow isn’t of too much help, as developer relations from a company called Stormpath loved plugging their IaaS startup on every imaginable post regarding this. <a href="https://docs.stormpath.com/client-api/product-guide/latest/password_reset.html" data-href="https://docs.stormpath.com/client-api/product-guide/latest/password_reset.html" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Their documentation also popped up everywhere</a> and they have <a href="https://stormpath.com/blog/the-pain-of-password-reset" data-href="https://stormpath.com/blog/the-pain-of-password-reset" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">a blogvertisement on password reset, as well</a>. However, all of this is for naught as Stormpath is defunct, <a href="https://stormpath.com/" data-href="https://stormpath.com/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">and it shuts down entirely</a> August 17, 2017.</p>
           <p name="6732" id="6732" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Alright, back to Google, for the only tutorial that seems to exist out there. We’ll take <a href="http://sahatyalkabov.com/how-to-implement-password-reset-in-nodejs/" data-href="http://sahatyalkabov.com/how-to-implement-password-reset-in-nodejs/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">the first result</a> for the Google search <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">express passport password reset. </em>Here is our old friend <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">bcrypt</em> again, with an even smaller cost factor of 5 used in the text, which is <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">far</em> too small of a cost factor for modern use.</p>
           <p name="115d" id="115d" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">However, this tutorial is pretty solid compared to others in that it uses <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">crypto.randomBytes</em> to generate truly random tokens, and expires them if they aren’t used. However #2 and #4 of the practices above aren’t honored by this comprehensive tutorial, and thus the password tokens themselves are vulnerable to authentication mistake number one, credential storage.</p>
           <p name="b639" id="b639" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Thankfully, this is of limited use thanks to the reset expiry. However, these tokens are especially fun if an attacker has read access to the user objects in the DB via BSON injection or can access Mongo freely due to misconfiguration. The attacker can just issue password resets for every user, read the unencrypted tokens from the DB, and set their own passwords for user accounts instead of having to go through the costly process of dictionary attacks on bcrypt hashes with a GPU rig.</p>
           <h3 name="0560" id="0560" class="graf graf--h3 graf-after--p">Mistake three: API&nbsp;tokens</h3>
           <p name="0574" id="0574" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">API tokens are credentials. They are just as sensitive as passwords or reset tokens. Most every developer knows this and tries to hold their AWS keys, Twitter secrets, etc. close to their chest, however this doesn’t seem to transfer into the code being authored.</p>
           <p name="0b98" id="0b98" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Let’s use <a href="https://jwt.io/" data-href="https://jwt.io/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">JSON Web Tokens</a> for API credentials. Having a stateless, blacklistable, claimable token is better than the old API key/secret pattern that has been used for the better part of a decade. Perhaps our junior Node.js dev has heard of JWT somewhere before, or saw <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">passport-jwt</em> and decided to implement the JWT strategy. In any case, JWT is where everyone seems to be moving in the Node.js sphere of influence. (The venerable <a href="https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=13866883" data-href="https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=13866883" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Thomas Ptacek will argue that JWT is bad</a> but I’m afraid that ship has sailed here.)</p>
           <p name="880e" id="880e" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">We’ll search for <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">express js jwt</em> on Google, and then find <a href="https://medium.com/@pandeysoni" data-href="https://medium.com/@pandeysoni" data-anchor-type="2" data-user-id="8d4c0a555bc3" data-action-value="8d4c0a555bc3" data-action="show-user-card" data-action-type="hover" class="markup--user markup--p-user" target="_blank">Soni Pandey</a>’s tutorial <a href="https://medium.com/@pandeysoni/user-authentication-using-jwt-json-web-token-in-node-js-using-express-framework-543151a38ea1" data-href="https://medium.com/@pandeysoni/user-authentication-using-jwt-json-web-token-in-node-js-using-express-framework-543151a38ea1" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">User Authentication using JWT (JSON Web Token) in Node.js</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> </em>which is the first tutorial result. Unfortunately, this doesn’t actually help us at all, since it doesn’t use Passport, but while we’re here we’ll quickly note the mistakes in credential storage:</p>
           <ol class="postList">
            <li name="692f" id="692f" class="graf graf--li graf-after--p">We’ll store the <a href="https://github.com/pandeysoni/User-Authentication-using-JWT-JSON-Web-Token-in-Node.js-Express/blob/master/server/config/config.js#L13" data-href="https://github.com/pandeysoni/User-Authentication-using-JWT-JSON-Web-Token-in-Node.js-Express/blob/master/server/config/config.js#L13" class="markup--anchor markup--li-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">JWT key in plaintext in the repository</a>.</li>
            <li name="bebb" id="bebb" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">We’ll <a href="https://github.com/pandeysoni/User-Authentication-using-JWT-JSON-Web-Token-in-Node.js-Express/blob/master/server/config/common.js#L54" data-href="https://github.com/pandeysoni/User-Authentication-using-JWT-JSON-Web-Token-in-Node.js-Express/blob/master/server/config/common.js#L54" class="markup--anchor markup--li-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">use a symmetric cipher to store passwords</a>. This means that I can get the encryption key and decrypt all of the passwords in event of a breach. The encryption key is shared with the JWT secret.</li>
            <li name="c59e" id="c59e" class="graf graf--li graf-after--li">We’ll use AES-256-CTR for password storage. We shouldn’t be using AES to start, and this mode of operation doesn’t help. I am not sure why this mode specifically was chosen, but <a href="https://crypto.stackexchange.com/a/33861" data-href="https://crypto.stackexchange.com/a/33861" class="markup--anchor markup--li-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">the choice alone leaves the ciphertext malleable</a>.</li>
           </ol>
           <p name="d8c2" id="d8c2" class="graf graf--p graf-after--li">Welp. Let’s back out to Google and we find the next tutorial. Scotch, which did an OK job with password storage in their passport-local tutorial, <a href="https://github.com/scotch-io/node-token-authentication/blob/master/app/models/user.js#L7" data-href="https://github.com/scotch-io/node-token-authentication/blob/master/app/models/user.js#L7" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">just ignores what they told you before and stores the passwords in plaintext</a> for this example.</p>
           <p name="ee84" id="ee84" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Uh, we’ll give that a pass for brevity, but it doesn’t help the copypasta crew. That’s because more interestingly, this tutorial <a href="https://github.com/scotch-io/node-token-authentication/blob/master/server.js#L81" data-href="https://github.com/scotch-io/node-token-authentication/blob/master/server.js#L81" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">also serializes the mongoose User object into the JWT</a>.</p>
           <p name="2491" id="2491" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Let’s clone the Scotch tutorial repository, follow the instructions, and run it. After a <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">DeprecationWarning </em>or three from Mongoose, we can hit <a href="http://localhost:8080/setup" data-href="http://localhost:8080/setup" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">http://localhost:8080/setup</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em"> </em>to create the user, then get a token by posting to /api/authenticate with the default credentials of “Nick Cerminara” and “password”. A token is returned, as displayed from Postman.</p>
           <figure name="5fe8" id="5fe8" class="graf graf--figure graf-after--p">
            <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder is-locked" style="max-width: 700px; max-height: 172px;">
             <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder-fill" style="padding-bottom: 24.6%;"></div>
             <div class="progressiveMedia js-progressiveMedia graf-image" data-image-id="1*wvb2F4-Rx4I1ji2EJIyXZg.png" data-width="1034" data-height="254" data-action="zoom" data-action-value="1*wvb2F4-Rx4I1ji2EJIyXZg.png">
              <img src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/freeze/max/60/1*wvb2F4-Rx4I1ji2EJIyXZg.png?q=20" crossorigin="anonymous" class="progressiveMedia-thumbnail js-progressiveMedia-thumbnail">
              <canvas class="progressiveMedia-canvas js-progressiveMedia-canvas"></canvas>
              <img class="progressiveMedia-image js-progressiveMedia-image" data-src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*wvb2F4-Rx4I1ji2EJIyXZg.png">
              <noscript class="js-progressiveMedia-inner">
               <img class="progressiveMedia-noscript js-progressiveMedia-inner" src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*wvb2F4-Rx4I1ji2EJIyXZg.png">
              </noscript>
             </div>
            </div>
            <figcaption class="imageCaption">
             A JWT token returned from the Scotch tutorial.
            </figcaption>
           </figure>
           <p name="038d" id="038d" class="graf graf--p graf-after--figure">Note that JSON Web Tokens are signed, but not encrypted. That means that big blob between the two periods is a Base64-encoded object. Quickly decoding it, we get something interesting.</p>
           <figure name="fee7" id="fee7" class="graf graf--figure graf-after--p">
            <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder is-locked" style="max-width: 687px; max-height: 271px;">
             <div class="aspectRatioPlaceholder-fill" style="padding-bottom: 39.4%;"></div>
             <div class="progressiveMedia js-progressiveMedia graf-image" data-image-id="1*5KcDyNtIfWXVe9uVUD0A_g.png" data-width="687" data-height="271" data-is-featured="true">
              <img src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/freeze/max/60/1*5KcDyNtIfWXVe9uVUD0A_g.png?q=20" crossorigin="anonymous" class="progressiveMedia-thumbnail js-progressiveMedia-thumbnail">
              <canvas class="progressiveMedia-canvas js-progressiveMedia-canvas"></canvas>
              <img class="progressiveMedia-image js-progressiveMedia-image" data-src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*5KcDyNtIfWXVe9uVUD0A_g.png">
              <noscript class="js-progressiveMedia-inner">
               <img class="progressiveMedia-noscript js-progressiveMedia-inner" src="https://cdn-images-1.medium.com/max/1600/1*5KcDyNtIfWXVe9uVUD0A_g.png">
              </noscript>
             </div>
            </div>
            <figcaption class="imageCaption">
             I love my passwords in plaintext in&nbsp;tokens.
            </figcaption>
           </figure>
           <p name="4275" id="4275" class="graf graf--p graf-after--figure">Now, anyone that has <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">even an expired token</em> has your password, as well as whatever else is stored in the Mongoose model. Given that this one came over HTTP, I could have sniffed it off of the wire.</p>
           <p name="e560" id="e560" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">What about the next tutorial? The next tutorial, <a href="https://jonathanmh.com/express-passport-json-web-token-jwt-authentication-beginners/" data-href="https://jonathanmh.com/express-passport-json-web-token-jwt-authentication-beginners/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">Express, Passport and JSON Web Token (jwt) Authentication for Beginners</em></a><em class="markup--em markup--p-em">, </em>contains the same information disclosure vulnerability<em class="markup--em markup--p-em">. </em>The next tutorial from a startup called <a href="http://blog.slatepeak.com/creating-a-simple-node-express-api-authentication-system-with-passport-and-jwt/" data-href="http://blog.slatepeak.com/creating-a-simple-node-express-api-authentication-system-with-passport-and-jwt/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">SlatePeak does the same serialization</a>. At this point, I gave up looking.</p>
           <h3 name="d577" id="d577" class="graf graf--h3 graf-after--p">Mistake four: rate&nbsp;limiting</h3>
           <p name="ee23" id="ee23" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">As I alluded to above, I did not find a mention of rate limiting or account locking in any of these authentication tutorials.</p>
           <p name="2017" id="2017" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Without rate limiting, an adversary can perform online dictionary attacks in which a tool like <a href="https://portswigger.net/burp/help/intruder_using.html" data-href="https://portswigger.net/burp/help/intruder_using.html" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Burp Intruder</a> is run in hopes of gaining access to an account with a weak password. Account lockout also helps with this problem by requiring extended login information from a user the next time they log in.</p>
           <p name="f8e4" id="f8e4" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">Remember, rate limiting also helps availability. <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">bcrypt</em> is a CPU-intensive function, and without rate limiting functions using bcrypt become an application-level denial of service vector, especially at high work factors. Multiple requests for user registration or login password checking are an easy way to turn a lightweight HTTP request into costly time for your server.</p>
           <p name="8de9" id="8de9" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">While I do not have a tutorial I can point to for these, there are tons of rate limiting middlewares for Express, such as <a href="https://github.com/nfriedly/express-rate-limit" data-href="https://github.com/nfriedly/express-rate-limit" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">express-rate-limit</a>, <a href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/express-limiter" data-href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/express-limiter" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">express-limiter</a>, and <a href="https://github.com/AdamPflug/express-brute" data-href="https://github.com/AdamPflug/express-brute" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">express-brute</a>. I cannot speak to the security of these modules and have not even looked at them; generally I <a href="https://expressjs.com/en/advanced/best-practice-performance.html#use-a-reverse-proxy" data-href="https://expressjs.com/en/advanced/best-practice-performance.html#use-a-reverse-proxy" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">recommend running a reverse proxy in production</a> and allowing <a href="https://www.nginx.com/blog/rate-limiting-nginx/" data-href="https://www.nginx.com/blog/rate-limiting-nginx/" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">rate limiting to requests to be handled by nginx</a> or whatever your load balancer is.</p>
           <h3 name="f0ef" id="f0ef" class="graf graf--h3 graf-after--p">Authentication is&nbsp;hard.</h3>
           <p name="d86a" id="d86a" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h3">I’m sure the tutorial developers will defend themselves with “This is just meant to explain the basics! Surely nobody will do this in production!” However, I cannot emphasize enough <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">just how false this is</em>. This is <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">especially</em> true when code is put out there in your tutorials. People will take your word for it?—?after all, you <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">do</em> have more expertise than they do.</p>
           <p name="177b" id="177b" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p"><strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">If you’re a beginner, don’t trust your tutorials.</strong> Copypasta from tutorials <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">will</em> likely get you, your company, and your clients in authentication trouble in the Node.js world. If you really need strong, production-ready, all-in-one authentication libraries, go back to something that holds your hand better, has better stability, and is more proven, like Rails/Devise.</p>
           <p name="7055" id="7055" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">The Node.js ecosystem, while accessible, still has a lot of sharp edges for JavaScript-based developers needing to write production web applications in a hurry. If you have a front-end background and don’t know other programming languages, I personally believe it is easier to pick up Ruby and stand on the shoulders of giants than it is to quickly learn how not to shoot yourself in the foot when writing these types of things from scratch.</p>
           <p name="2398" id="2398" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">If you’re a tutorial writer, <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">please</em> update your tutorials, <em class="markup--em markup--p-em">especially </em>the boilerplate code. This code will become copypasta into others’ production web applications.</p>
           <p name="8f2e" id="8f2e" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">If you are a die-hard Node.js developer, hopefully you’ve learned a few things not to do in your authentication system you’re rolling with Passport. You will likely get something wrong. I haven’t gotten close to covering all of the ways to get it wrong in this one post. It shouldn’t be your job to roll your own auth for your Express application. There should be something better.</p>
           <p name="634f" id="634f" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">If you’re interested in better securing the Node ecosystem, please DM me <a href="https://twitter.com/_micaksica" data-href="https://twitter.com/_micaksica" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">@_micaksica</a> on Twitter.</p>
           <blockquote name="9540" id="9540" class="graf graf--blockquote graf-after--p">
            This post was brought to you by espresso because I’m out of sake.
           </blockquote>
           <h4 name="18c2" id="18c2" class="graf graf--h4 graf-after--blockquote">Addendum (last updated August 10,&nbsp;2017)</h4>
           <p name="1bbf" id="1bbf" class="graf graf--p graf-after--h4">This post started out of frustration from the problems I had been seeing, and has somehow taken on a life of its own. It has received more responses, more feedback, and caused more controversy than I had expected it to. Clearly this has been a point of frustration for other developers.</p>
           <p name="aabc" id="aabc" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">If you find errors in this post, please message me and we’ll work to fix them. What we don’t need is another misguided post. That’s the opposite of what we need.</p>
           <p name="da6c" id="da6c" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p">However, I implore you: let’s not just work on this post. Let’s make the ecosystem better in the future. If you find errors or have other practices, things you’ve learned in production, or simply improvements, feel free to DM me. <strong class="markup--strong markup--p-strong">It seems that the next best step for us is to write a living document</strong> containing “cheat sheet” style best practices around authentication with Node/Express as a patch until there are proven, robust solutions.</p>
           <p name="1022" id="1022" class="graf graf--p graf-after--p graf--trailing">To that end, I’ve started <a href="https://gitlab.com/micaksica/nodeauth-best-practices" data-href="https://gitlab.com/micaksica/nodeauth-best-practices" class="markup--anchor markup--p-anchor" rel="noopener" target="_blank">a repository on GitLab</a>. Feel free to file issues, your recommendations, or make some pull requests to get us started. I’ll work on it as soon as I get a chance this upcoming weekend.</p>
          </div>
         </div>
        </section>
